10.1016/j.jmp.2010.01.003
Discriminability of pure tones on the basis of intensity steadily improves with level, inasmuch as the Weber fraction  
                         Δ 
                         x 
                         / 
                         x 
                       decreases with increasing intensity  
                         x 
                      . The data are linear when plotted in log–log coordinates, with a slope that is slightly, but distinctly, less than zero. This observable property has been given the name  near-miss to Weber’s law  ( McGill & Goldberg, 1968a,b ). A theoretical account of the near-miss data was proposed by  Falmagne (1985)  in the form of a power law (see also  Iverson, 2006b ), with a special emphasis on the interpretation of the exponent, in the framework of a  subtractive representation  for the choice probabilities ( Falmagne, 1994 ) which is a generalization of the well-studied  Fechnerian representation . The main goal of this article is to propose a generalization of the subtractive representation and to study the conditions that force such a representation to degenerate to a subtractive representation or even a Fechnerian one. These conditions include the  balance condition  on the choice probabilities, the more relaxed condition of  weakly balanced  probabilities, and  Falmagne’s power law . Our main theoretical conclusions follow from the solution of functional equations.

A related topic concerns the  Fechner method . Fechner is famous for constructing a scale by integrating just noticeable differences (jnds). Assuming we have the  Weber sensitivities  from a weakly balanced affine representation, is it possible to generalize the Fechner method so as to recover the scales that occur in the affine representation? We show that this generalization exists, and we illustrate it on (idealized) near-miss data. This part of the work extends the study by  Iverson (2006a) .

We confine our discussion to discrimination choice probabilities. However, much of what we say about discrimination applies in a natural way to other forms of data.

The paper is organized as follows. In Section  2 , we give a brief introduction to the near-miss to Weber’s law and remind readers of the balance condition. In Section  3 , we propose a generalization of the subtractive representation, which we call an  affine representation . We devote Section  4  to our main results on affine representations. We first investigate the uniqueness of an affine representation. We then study when an affine representation is subtractive. We find, not too surprisingly perhaps, that affine representations remain closely related to subtractive representations. Indeed, in the presence of seemingly innocuous side conditions, affine representations degenerate to subtractive ones. In particular, we show that the balance condition forces an affine representation to be Fechnerian. Further, we show that Falmagne’s power law takes on a special form within a weakly balanced system of probabilities; again an affine representation reduces to a Fechnerian one. In Section  5 , we show how the Fechner method (of integrating jnds) can be generalized to construct the scales that appear in a weakly balanced affine representation.

Experimental evidence does not often support Weber’s law. Accordingly, a more general formulation, which states that

                       This hypothesis has been examined in several empirical studies. For example,  Jesteadt, Wier, and Green (1977)  found that the ability to discriminate pure tones of the same frequency on the basis of loudness improves steadily with increasing tone intensity. In particular, they found that the logarithm of the Weber fraction  
                         Δ 
                         
                            ( 
                            y 
                            ) 
                         
                         / 
                         y 
                       is linearly related to the logarithm of the intensity  
                         y 
                      , with a slope slightly less than 0, indicating a systematic deviation from Weber’s law in agreement with hypothesis  (1) . See  Doble et al. (2003)  for a compilation of other experimental studies in which  (1)  was proposed.

Other studies, though, have shown that the formulation in  (1)  does not adequately account for near-miss data, in that the exponent parameter  
                         α 
                       may depend on

The study of the near-miss phenomenon is impacted by the so-called  balance condition  ( Falmagne, 1985 ):  
                         
                            (5) 
                            
                               P 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  x 
                                  , 
                                  y 
                                  ) 
                               
                               + 
                               P 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  y 
                                  , 
                                  x 
                                  ) 
                               
                               = 
                               1 
                               . 
                            
                         
                       This condition is enforced automatically either by experimental design or whenever the common practice of averaging data over order in a two-alternative forced-choice task is adopted.  Doble et al. (2003)  examined the effect of the balance condition on two forms of theoretical interest for near-miss data, one based on  (2)  and the other based on  (3) . They found that a logical inconsistency exists when using the formulation  (2) .

One can relax the balance condition. Following  Falmagne (1982)  on “anchored” psychometric families (see also  Augustin, 2008 ), we adopt the concept of a weakly balanced system.

                       Clearly, the balance condition implies that

                       is weakly balanced, but not vice versa. A related idea has been discussed at length by  Dzhafarov (2002b, 2003)  in his theory of greater–less and same–different judgments in connection with the notion of regular minimality. In the next section, we introduce affine representations and show that regular systems of discrimination probabilities can always be weakly balanced within the framework provided by affine representations.

In discrimination studies (e.g., a forced-choice paradigm in which a participant’s task is to select which of two visual stimuli is brighter), a standard  Fechnerian representation  has been proposed for the choice probabilities (see  Falmagne, 1985 , for a general discussion). It takes the form  
                         
                            (6) 
                            
                               P 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  x 
                                  , 
                                  y 
                                  ) 
                               
                               = 
                               F 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  u 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     x 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                  − 
                                  u 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     y 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                               , 
                            
                         
                       in which the scales  
                         u 
                       and  
                         F 
                       are continuous and strictly increasing on a common interval of the positive reals. Such a representation postulates that the sensory mechanism produces an internal scale that evaluates physical stimuli as real-valued quantities, and that the discrimination probability is determined by the magnitude of the difference between the two quantities. This representation subsumes a family of models of discrimination. For example, it generalizes Case V of Thurstone’s Law of Comparative Judgment.  Iverson (1979)  studied the conditions under which Thurstone Case III representations are also Fechnerian. For a more general treatment of such a probability-distance hypothesis in the framework of multidimensional Fechnerian scaling; see  Dzhafarov (2002a) .

Falmagne (1982)  generalized the Fechnerian representation to situations in which (the internal scales of) a signal  
                         x 
                       and a referent  
                         y 
                       may reflect different attributes:  
                         
                            (7) 
                            
                               P 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  x 
                                  , 
                                  y 
                                  ) 
                               
                               = 
                               F 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  u 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     x 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                  − 
                                  g 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     y 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                               , 
                            
                         
                       in which all the functions in the right member are continuous and strictly increasing. Following  Falmagne (1982) , we call  (7)  a  subtractive representation .

We now propose an affine representation, which is a generalization of the subtractive representation  (7) . Two forms of such a representation are of interest.

Affine representations have been studied in the past. In particular,  Levine (1970, 1972)  characterized affine representations by studying iteration groups that are represented as a family of linear functions closed under composition of functions. In special families of lines, the members are parallel to one another. This “degenerate” circumstance deserves special attention and we look closely at degenerate representations in Section  4 .

The assumption of a regular or a smooth system of discrimination probabilities (see  Definition 1 ) puts strong constraints on the functions in  (8) and (9) . In this respect we note the following properties of the various scales that comprise an affine representation. If

Two types of gain-control representations have been proposed in the past. In the first type, it is assumed that the participant makes a decision based on the use of a sensory device similar to that of an automatic gain control. Such a device adjusts its “gain” based on the ratio between the signal strength and the joint input of the background and the embedded signal. This representation was proposed by  Iverson and Pavel (1981)  in a study of masked loudness matching in noisy backgrounds, in which it was assumed that the overall effect of noise is to suppress the perceived loudness of an embedded signal. This type of gain-control representation, when used to represent discrimination probabilities, takes the form

                                  F

                                              x 
                                              ) 
                                           
                                        
                                        
                                           w

                                              x 
                                              )

                                           v 
                                           
                                              ( 
                                              y 
                                              )

                          in which the functions  
                            F 
                         ,  
                            r 
                         ,  
                            w 
                         , and  
                            v 
                          are continuous and strictly increasing.

The impact of homogeneity laws on affine representations has been noted in the literature. For example, the study of partial masking by  Pavel and Iverson (1981)  revealed and exploited an empirical property of data, called  shift invariance , which they showed constrained the analytical form of the scales comprising an affine representation ( Iverson & Pavel, 1981 ). Another example involves the near-miss data of  Doble et al. (2006) , discussed after  Theorem 10 . As will be shown, affine representations in the presence of homogeneity laws can degenerate to subtractive ones.

We noted at the end of Section  2  that a regular system of discrimination probabilities with an affine representation can always be weakly balanced. To see this, suppose that a regular system of discrimination probabilities

In the following section, we investigate several conditions under which affine representations degenerate to subtractive ones.

For convenience, we focus on the affine representation  (8) . All of the theoretical results presented in this paper apply to  (9)  by similar arguments.

If an affine representation  (8)  is equivalent to a subtractive representation  (7) , we shall refer to it as  degenerate . We call an affine representation  (8) 
                      non-degenerate  if it cannot be reduced to the subtractive form  (7) . Note that if two affine representations exist for the same data, then one is non-degenerate (respectively degenerate) if and only if the other is non-degenerate (respectively degenerate).

We first explore the conditions under which an affine representation  (8)  degenerates to a subtractive representation  (7) . Clearly, if  
                         h 
                       is constant, then  (8)  degenerates to  (7) . But this restriction does not completely characterize matters; if  
                         h 
                       is not constant but is linearly related to  
                         g 
                      , then  (8)  also reduces to  (7) . It turns out the above two conditions are also necessary for the reduction of  (8)  to  (7) .  Theorem 6  characterizes such degenerate representations.

We put this theoretical result to use in our derivation of  Theorems 8 and 11 , and  Corollary 13 .

We now investigate the uniqueness of an affine representation. We show that the functions  
                         u 
                       and  
                         h 
                       in  (8)  are respectively interval and ratio scales, and that the function  
                         F 
                       is a member of a location-scale family.  Theorem 7  refers expressly to affine representations of the first kind. However, in view of  Theorem 4 , it is clear that a similar result obtains for affine representations of the second kind.

As described in the previous section, instances of the two affine representations  (8) and (9)  have been used in different experimental situations. We now ask the following natural question: Can the two affine representations  (8) ,  (9)  co-exist for data from the same experiment? The answer is yes, but only under special forms of all the functions involved.

Theorem 8  finds an application when an affine representation  (8)  satisfies the balance condition. Indeed,  Theorem 10  is a corollary of  Theorem 8 . We begin with a lemma.

Theorem 10 
                         
                            Suppose that a regular system of discrimination probabilities  
                            
                               P 
                             
                            satisfies an affine representation 
                            (8) 
                            , in which the function  
                            
                               h 
                             
                            is either constant or strictly monotonic. Suppose that the balance condition 
                            (5) 
                            holds for these probabilities. Then the representation is Fechnerian.

Suppose that a regular system of discrimination probabilities  
                            
                               P 
                             
                            satisfies an affine representation 
                            (8) 
                            , in which the function  
                            
                               h 
                             
                            is either constant or strictly monotonic. Suppose that the balance condition 
                            (5) 
                            holds for these probabilities. Then the representation is Fechnerian.

Proof 
                         If the function  
                               h 
                             is constant in the representation  (8) , then the representation is subtractive and  Lemma 9  applies. Consequently, it is enough to assume that  
                               h 
                             is strictly monotonic. The balance condition implies that  
                               
                                  
                                     F 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        u 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           x 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        h 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           y 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        + 
                                        g 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           y 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     = 
                                     P 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        x 
                                        , 
                                        y 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     = 
                                     1 
                                     − 
                                     P 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        y 
                                        , 
                                        x 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     = 
                                     1 
                                     − 
                                     F 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        u 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           y 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        h 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           x 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        + 
                                        g 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           x 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     = 
                                     
                                        
                                           F 
                                        
                                        
                                           ̃ 
                                        
                                     
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        u 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           y 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        h 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           x 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        + 
                                        g 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           x 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     . 
                                  
                               
                            
                            Theorem 8  applies to reveal that  
                               P 
                             satisfies a subtractive representation.  Lemma 9  then applies to show that this subtractive representation is in fact Fechnerian.  □

If the function  
                               h 
                             is constant in the representation  (8) , then the representation is subtractive and  Lemma 9  applies. Consequently, it is enough to assume that  
                               h 
                             is strictly monotonic. The balance condition implies that  
                               
                                  
                                     F 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        u 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           x 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        h 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           y 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        + 
                                        g 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           y 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     = 
                                     P 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        x 
                                        , 
                                        y 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     = 
                                     1 
                                     − 
                                     P 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        y 
                                        , 
                                        x 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     = 
                                     1 
                                     − 
                                     F 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        u 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           y 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        h 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           x 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        + 
                                        g 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           x 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     = 
                                     
                                        
                                           F 
                                        
                                        
                                           ̃ 
                                        
                                     
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        u 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           y 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        h 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           x 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        + 
                                        g 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           x 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     . 
                                  
                               
                            
                            Theorem 8  applies to reveal that  
                               P 
                             satisfies a subtractive representation.  Lemma 9  then applies to show that this subtractive representation is in fact Fechnerian.  □

Thus the balance condition that is often assumed to hold for near-miss data ( Doble et al., 2003 ) furnishes a concrete application of  Theorems 8 and 10 , in which the constraint on the discrimination probabilities forces the affine representation  (8)  to be, in fact, Fechnerian. One might wonder what happens if we only assume a weakly balanced system of probabilities. In fact, we have shown in the proof of  Theorem 5  that the affine representation  (8)  within a weakly balanced system admits a special form (see Eq.  (14) ):

We mention that a closely related result similar to  Theorem 11  was reported in  Falmagne and Lundberg (2000) . Without assuming weakly balanced probabilities, they showed that Falmagne’s power law  (4)  implies that  
                         w 
                      ,  
                         v 
                      , and  
                         r 
                       in the gain-control type representation of form  (11)  are all logarithmic functions (see Theorem 2.6 on p. 206 of  Falmagne and Lundberg (2000) ). One important consequence, which was not pointed out explicitly by those authors, is that their results show that  
                         w 
                       and  
                         r 
                       are linearly related, indicating that  (11)  can be reformulated as a subtractive representation. It is not hard to extend  Theorem 11  to show that, in the context of Falmagne’s power law  (4) , the affine representation  (8)  degenerates to a subtractive representation. Moreover, the functional forms in  (8)  can be determined. 
                         7 
                      
                      
                         7 
                         The Falmagne–Lundberg theorem also can be generalized by taking into account the following three differences: (i)  Falmagne and Lundberg (2000)  assumed a gain-control type representation  (11) , which is an instance of the affine representation  (9) . (ii)  Falmagne and Lundberg (2000)  formulated Falmagne’s power law as a function of the signal but not of the background/referent. (iii) We can relax the assumption of the monotonicity of the parameters  
                               β 
                             and  
                               h 
                             in  Falmagne and Lundberg (2000)  to cover more cases, including the ones consistent with empirical findings. 
                       We do not go into details here. These results can also be used to help clarify the functional forms outlined in other closely related studies such as the one by  Augustin (2008) .

We have confined our discussion of the affine representation to discrimination choice probabilities. But relevant data are not always probabilities. For example, the loudness matching functions of  Pavel and Iverson (1981)  are measurements in units of physical intensity, and the threshold contrast ratios of  Lu and Dosher (2008)  are ratios of  
                         
                            
                               d 
                            
                            
                               ′ 
                            
                         
                      . Some of the theory we have developed here can be extended in a natural way to apply to those data as well.

One might think that, since the balance condition is quite natural, it must be ubiquitous, and so affine representations would not be so interesting. We point out that the balance condition does not always hold. There is a substantial literature on time order effects, which result when, for example, the stimulus in the first interval of a two-interval task is judged consistently more intense than the stimulus in the second interval, even when the two have identical intensity ( Hellström, 1979 ). In such cases, if no averaging over order of stimulus presentation is done, the balance condition will not hold. Because averaging over order of presentation automatically enforces the balance condition (see  Doble et al., 2003 , Appendix A), important aspects of the data may be lost; for this reason, authors have warned against averaging data in two-alternative forced-choice tasks ( Doble et al., 2003; Falmagne, 1985, 1994; Hellström, 1985; Ulrich & Vorberg, 2009; Yeshurun, Carrasco, & Maloney, 2008 ). In the two examples in the previous paragraph, no balance condition was enforced. In such cases an affine representation will not automatically reduce to a Fechnerian one (see, for example,  Iverson & Pavel, 1981 ).

Furthermore, even if one knows that an affine representation is degenerate (e.g., the balance condition holds), it might be the case that the affine interpretation is preferred to the difference representation. For example, the degenerate affine representation requires that a random representation has mean linear in standard deviation. That interpretation is no longer available in a difference representation.

Recently,  Iverson (2006b)  introduced a (psychophysical)  law of similarity :

Such a characterization of observable properties is very general and includes, for example, Falmagne’s power law  (4) , shift invariance in loudness matching ( Iverson & Pavel, 1981 ), and the threshold contrast ratios ( Lu & Dosher, 2008 ) as special cases. In the framework provided by a Fechnerian representation,  Iverson (2006b)  characterized all possible functional forms for the sensitivities

                       (or

                      ). An interesting follow-up study is thus to examine the impact of the law of similarity  (46)  on an affine representation.
